  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_43688710-4a74-4e4a-979d-84c199ff35dc'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer16042082781130837029"><br/><i>When the last dragon and the last elf break the circle, the past and the future will meet, and the sun of a new summer will shine in the sky.</i><br/><br/>In a world shrouded in darkness and continually lashed by rain, a young elf named Yorsh struggles to survive.   <span id="freeText16042082781130837029" style="display:none"><br /><i>When the last dragon and the last elf break the circle, the past and the future will meet, and the sun of a new summer will shine in the sky.</i><br /><br />In a world shrouded in darkness and continually lashed by rain, a young elf named Yorsh struggles to survive. His village has been destroyed by the torrential waters, leaving Yorsh suddenly orphaned and alone-the earth's last elf. But soon Yorsh discovers he is part of a powerful prophecy to save the world from the Dark Age that has begun. First, however, the young elf will have to find another orphaned creature-the world's last dragon.<br /><br />Full of great tenderness and humor, this magical journey tells the story of a world plagued by intolerance and wickedness, and the elf and the dragon who will fight for its redemption and bring it back into the light.<br /></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_623817" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/623817" />
    <a title="Kimberly Hirsh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51472-kimberly-hirsh"><img alt="Kimberly Hirsh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438042231p2/51472.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/623817?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kimberly Hirsh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kimberly Hirsh" href="/user/show/51472-kimberly-hirsh">Kimberly Hirsh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer623817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3397519758533328513"><em>When you have too much sadness, the magic drowns in it, like people in water.  If you think things hard enough, they become true.  But if you have sadness inside, all that comes out of your head is sadness.  </em>(p. 35)<br/><br/>Sometimes a book finds you at the right time.  That's what happened to me with Silvana de Mari's <em>The Last Dragon</em>.  I was in the middle of reading this book when I lost a friend of mine to her own mental illness.  It was exactly the book I needed at that time.<br/><br/>Yorshkrunsquarkljolnerstri</span>
  <span id="freeText3397519758533328513" style="display:none"><em>When you have too much sadness, the magic drowns in it, like people in water.  If you think things hard enough, they become true.  But if you have sadness inside, all that comes out of your head is sadness.  </em>(p. 35)<br /><br />Sometimes a book finds you at the right time.  That's what happened to me with Silvana de Mari's <em>The Last Dragon</em>.  I was in the middle of reading this book when I lost a friend of mine to her own mental illness.  It was exactly the book I needed at that time.<br /><br />Yorshkrunsquarkljolnerstri, "Yorsh" for short, is the last elf.  He lost his mother at a young age, and his grandmother sent him away while she remained in their house and drowned.  Though he is one born lately, as he so often reminds his companions, he has already experienced much misery.  As Yorsh and the two humans he meets travel through the city of Daligar, he reads a prophecy concerning the last dragon and the last elf breaking the circle.  He immediately recognizes himself as the last elf, and knows he must find the last dragon.  Armed with his father's traveling map and the support of two humans shunned for helping him, Yorsh sets out to find this last dragon and break the circle.<br /><br />This book strikes a delicate balance between pathos and humor.  Yorsh's disdain for what he perceives as human lack of intelligence is juxtaposed with his own naivete, leading to misunderstandings that while intended to be funny, could become grating if the book relied on them exclusively for its humor.  Fortunately, this sort of comedy is just embellishment on a book that is of great substance.  As Yorsh grows, he learns about the world around him, and his eyes are opened.<br /><br />At the heart of the book is the idea that you cannot trust your own preconceived notions about people you've never met.  Yorsh's ideas about humans, humans' ideas about elves, and everyone's ideas about dragons turn out to be extremely off-base.  Around this theme, Silvana de Mari builds a world populated with characters both endearing and terrifying.  This is a dystopian society, but its children live lives filled with hope, despite their desperate conditions.  Yorsh, the last dragon, and these children unite to change their world for the better.<br /><br />While <em>The Last Dragon </em>gets off to a slow start, its characters are so touching that it's worth it to read all the way to the end.  Yorsh and his companions are darlings, and you want to see how they fare in their quest to improve their world.  I would recommend this book to lovers of fantasy, as well as readers who may need some hope in a dark time of life.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3397519758533328513" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1271940-robin?shelf=international">international</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer252847164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15359137229299774523">Translated from the Italian -- prose is beautifully done, would love to be able to read the original Italian. A story in two parts. Yorsh (short for Yorshkrunsquarkljolnerstrink) is a little elf, one born lately, alone in a world where elves are persecuted. Indeed, he is the last elf. A young woman and a hunter gradually befriend him, helping him follow his father's last instructions and make his way to the last dragon.   <span id="freeText15359137229299774523" style="display:none">Translated from the Italian -- prose is beautifully done, would love to be able to read the original Italian. A story in two parts. Yorsh (short for Yorshkrunsquarkljolnerstrink) is a little elf, one born lately, alone in a world where elves are persecuted. Indeed, he is the last elf. A young woman and a hunter gradually befriend him, helping him follow his father's last instructions and make his way to the last dragon. They find him in a cave filled with books -- the accumulation of the world's knowledge. Turns out it was the "great library of the Second Runic Dynasty. It was a time of knowledge, and people treated this place as though it were a temple: in silence, and no spitting, with clean hands, and the dust wiped from their shoes. And to be quite certain that nobody misbehaved, dragons have always been here, and thet's why there are signs that say 'Here Be Dragons.' This was the biggest collection of knowledge that had ever existed. Then men lost writing. They forgot how to read. Barbarism flooded the world. the very memory of this place faded away. Many people never believed in its existence, but with my wings, I finally found it. And when I reached this place, great was my joy. All the books in the world were for me." (103)<br /><br />The second part of the story begins about thirteen years later. Yorsh has read all the books and kept the last dragon company all these years, though the dragon grows more irascible and grumpy. Turns out he/she was brooding. When the young dragon hatches, Yorsh cares for it, even teaching it to fly. But he also longs for companionship and needs new clothes, so he sets out to find Sajra and Moser, the humans who had helped him before. In their village he finds desolation, but evidence they had a child. Robi's story is told in parallel, and eventually they come together. There is a fair amount of adventure and danger set in both tales, but the overall mood is more introspective. The varying perspectives of the elf, humans and dragon are convincingly portrayed. Elves do have some magic, but they are capable of great empathy -- they literally feel other's pain, so they don't make great warriors. The writing is beautiful, often infused with humor as characters misunderstand then get to know one another. It's lovely to watch characters that grow and change -- even when they were already perfect, as in the case of the dragon.<br /><br />For readers who enjoy fantasy and stories about dragons. Fans of Cornelia Funke (Thief Lord, Dragon Rider), Anne McCaffrey's Pern books, will enjoy this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15359137229299774523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5644056787636723753">This has to be my favorite book of all time. It's the one that introduced me to the world of fantasy. Full of humor, bravery, love, sorrow, and magic, this book is, in my opinion, well-rounded. I can read it no matter what mood I'm in, and it's always amazing.<br/><br/>I find De Mari's writing style to be, well, brilliant. I especially love the repetition of certain words.   <span id="freeText5644056787636723753" style="display:none">This has to be my favorite book of all time. It's the one that introduced me to the world of fantasy. Full of humor, bravery, love, sorrow, and magic, this book is, in my opinion, well-rounded. I can read it no matter what mood I'm in, and it's always amazing.<br /><br />I find De Mari's writing style to be, well, brilliant. I especially love the repetition of certain words. The style changes as she narrates each character, making it seem from their view without using first person.<br /><br />Speaking of characters, De Mari's are beautiful. Yorsh, the elf, is witty and pure, and Robi is strong-willed yet very lovable. Ebrow, the dragon, is majestic, self-centered, and absolutely wonderful. The dialogue between characters is practically perfect.<br /><br />In my opinion, this is one of the most outstanding and unique books I've read.<br /><br />If I were to rate this like a movie, it would be PG.</span>

  <span id="freeText114794839806834134" style="display:none">This book is a surprise. It was a random pick from Big Bookshop sale before Ampang Park was demolished so it had been sitting at my TBR shelf for some time.   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3168166161" />
    <a title="Mbilmey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16705617-mbilmey"><img alt="Mbilmey" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3168166161?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mbilmey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mbilmey" href="/user/show/16705617-mbilmey">Mbilmey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3168166161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8011686560551652734">This book is bizarre. And it has such a weird sense of storytelling. The first half is like if the Mandalorian was told from the perspective of a very chatty Baby Yoda. You get to know Baby Yoda very well, but he's so young that everything else that's happening is a bit confusing and you don't get to know the other characters as well. And then you never see some of them again.<br/>Then, the book completely shifts gears, and suddenly it's a story split between little orphan Annie and How to Train Your</span>
  <span id="freeText8011686560551652734" style="display:none">This book is bizarre. And it has such a weird sense of storytelling. The first half is like if the Mandalorian was told from the perspective of a very chatty Baby Yoda. You get to know Baby Yoda very well, but he's so young that everything else that's happening is a bit confusing and you don't get to know the other characters as well. And then you never see some of them again.<br />Then, the book completely shifts gears, and suddenly it's a story split between little orphan Annie and How to Train Your dragon. <br />But it all comes together beautifully though Yorsh in an exciting, fantastical, but somehow practical finale.<br /><br />Weaknesses: There's a sheer weirdness to it, the historical aspects are muddy, a lot of difficulties in connecting with certain characters, the complete shift after the first half--mostly things due to the limited perspectives it's written in. There's a lot of this world that really could use more explanation or exploration.<br />Strengths: Yorshkrunsquarkljolnerstrink. That the writer managed to keep his personality consistent while aging him is extremely impressive. Also, I'm very attached. Erbrow Jr. is also great. I did not believe my friend who said so, but it's true. The relationships are also just really well done, even when you barely know their names. I was sooooooo attached to the hunter and the woman. The practical world building is also pretty great and fascinating.<br /><br />So, my French skills are not the greatest yet, but I may be polishing them up so I can read more because for some reason this is the only of her books in English. :(<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8011686560551652734" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Dita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8397590-dita"><img alt="Dita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589920606p2/8397590.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/444832301?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dita" href="/user/show/8397590-dita">Dita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer444832301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6426873699865575019">There are only like about three books that have ever made me cry at some point throughout the story. This is one of those books. <br/><br/>The author has the power to build the story so that it would make you laugh out loud and also cry, but that does not make the story less believable. It is set in the human world that seems to be something a lot like the medieval times where mystical creatures such as elves and dragons are said to be extinct, but turns out they`re not. The story consists of both some pr</span>
  <span id="freeText6426873699865575019" style="display:none">There are only like about three books that have ever made me cry at some point throughout the story. This is one of those books. <br /><br />The author has the power to build the story so that it would make you laugh out loud and also cry, but that does not make the story less believable. It is set in the human world that seems to be something a lot like the medieval times where mystical creatures such as elves and dragons are said to be extinct, but turns out they`re not. The story consists of both some pretty dark, depressive, sad and some pretty beautiful, happy parts (also hilarious ones), but despite everything it always gives that feeling of “it gets better” and hope. <br /><br />All the characters are believable and very well developed; it is not possible to not root for the main characters or ignore their lives. In my opinion the main strength of this book is that it can be read by both children and adults. Generally it has the feeling of a beautiful fantasy fairytale yet there are no moments it is too violent or complicated for children or too childish and shallow for adults. It is a perfect book that teaches children to see the real wealth and values of human life (love, family, friendship, etc.), at the same time it reminds adults what has the real meaning in our lives. I loved this book to pieces because of its simplicity - it is one of the most touching and heartwarming books I`ve ever read. <br /><br />I`d advise this book to everyone who is not afraid of fairytales and some pretty good fantasy. I believe those readers who had enjoyed Harry Potter series, Narnia series, LOTR series, Spiderwick chronicles, etc. would also enjoy this book as much as I did.<br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13801956804621144456">The Last Dragon by Silvana DeMari is a good pick for the 4-6 grades: Attention elf and dragon lovers! Here is a book for you! Yorsh is a young elf… just a baby really. He is wandering the earth looking for food and shelter. A woman comes to his aide and shares her food with him, even though there are strict laws against harboring elves. The two of them soon find themselves in trouble and are rescued by a hunter. The elf accidentally sees an ancient prophecy written on a wall saying that the last</span>
  <span id="freeText13801956804621144456" style="display:none">The Last Dragon by Silvana DeMari is a good pick for the 4-6 grades: Attention elf and dragon lovers! Here is a book for you! Yorsh is a young elf… just a baby really. He is wandering the earth looking for food and shelter. A woman comes to his aide and shares her food with him, even though there are strict laws against harboring elves. The two of them soon find themselves in trouble and are rescued by a hunter. The elf accidentally sees an ancient prophecy written on a wall saying that the last elf and the last dragon must meet and break the circle so that all the misery on earth may end. Yorsh is off on an odyssey. He finds the last dragon and cares for him for years. As the old dragon dies, something special happens. A new dragon is born! With his new friend’s help, Yorsh goes out into the world to find the daughter of the woman and hunter who had saved him many years ago. He dose find Rori, the daughter of his friends, but he also finds a world turned upside down. He must lead the beleaguered humans in a last battle for their freedom. But at what cost? This is a tender story of selflessness. Many of the characters choose to risk their own comforts and put off their own desires to help and care for others. This book teaches selflessness and tenacity.<br /><br />Susan Huff Library Media Specialist</span>

  <span id="freeText3504978760696302440" style="display:none">I listened to this one and after 3 scratched copies, I finally was able to finish it.     <a title="Pandora " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1229414-pandora"><img alt="Pandora " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1214164106p2/1229414.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1229414-pandora?shelf=children-s">children-s</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132975626" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16431268462598005011">This is a two and half star book. Though there were elements I liked about the book there was also other elements I didn't like. For a children's fantasy I found the book too dark and depressing. It spends a long time with people being mean to each other. The book also dwells on death. The ending was also disappointing because I was hoping and expecting more of a fairy tale. It wasn't the ending was awful but, I was hoping for more especially with the build up. It was akin to Victor Hugo's The M</span>
  <span id="freeText16431268462598005011" style="display:none">This is a two and half star book. Though there were elements I liked about the book there was also other elements I didn't like. For a children's fantasy I found the book too dark and depressing. It spends a long time with people being mean to each other. The book also dwells on death. The ending was also disappointing because I was hoping and expecting more of a fairy tale. It wasn't the ending was awful but, I was hoping for more especially with the build up. It was akin to Victor Hugo's The Man Who Laughs which was another example of an ending not matching the begining of the book.<br /><br />This book is also difficult to get into at the begining as the elf is much younger than you think. The book also makes a jump in part II that was aburpt. The style also was more tell than show which might not work with Americans. <br /><br />It is difficult book. I can't recommend it as much as I would others. I doubt though the book will move without a good book talk.<br /><br />I did enjoy the characters and there were some good comic bits. Overall though the book just didn't work for me too well.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16431268462598005011" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer199994092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14826136798711105326">Yorsh is the last elf in the world after his village was lost in a flood, and has to fend for himself in the world. Later, he meets the last creature of another species, the powerful and wise dragon.<br/><br/>I did give this book a chance, but it may simply not be to my taste. It's target audience would be too young to read a book this size. I just did not like any of the characters. I kept waiting for a good character to come along with some sense in them. Yorsh generally embarrassed me most of the time,</span>
  <span id="freeText14826136798711105326" style="display:none">Yorsh is the last elf in the world after his village was lost in a flood, and has to fend for himself in the world. Later, he meets the last creature of another species, the powerful and wise dragon.<br /><br />I did give this book a chance, but it may simply not be to my taste. It's target audience would be too young to read a book this size. I just did not like any of the characters. I kept waiting for a good character to come along with some sense in them. Yorsh generally embarrassed me most of the time, painfully so, though admittedly he does gain some sense by the end. The dragon was only slightly better. After struggling through the book, I was disgusted with what seemed to me to be fluff. <br />For example, the wedding dress. For a large chunk of the book, Yorsh is wearing a wedding dress, veil and all. He just finds it, (and apparently the lace and intricate design don't give it way that maybe it might be for a girl) he puts it on without a second thought. Not only that, but correctly it seems. That just felt far-fetched to me, and like the author was trying too hard to be funny.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14826136798711105326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4359419035773136751">I was completely captivated by this wonderful little book.<br/><br/>The beginning is a bit grim, OK straight up grim—<br/>Once the quest starts and the dragon is found the reader is hooked. It slowly dawns on the engaging Yorsh that he truly is the last elf, from an immensely lonely state he finds the last dragon and then the action begins. Tackling themes of racism, courage, friendship and wit, this books has been translated into many languages. Originally Italian. I eagerly await a new title by DiMari.<br/><br/></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4310825470841048540">Now that's what I call a fantasy! It's got elves, dragons, magic, romance, Fate, even a treatise on how to name the family dog from which can be extrapolated how to name the family horse. I could put it down, but only just barely. Here's hoping that more of de Mari's work gets translated.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer388573450575195759"><p> <b> <blockquote> Warning : This is a bit of a rant review, so I have lots of things to say about later book events.   <span id="freeText388573450575195759" style="display:none"><p> <b> <blockquote> Warning : This is a bit of a rant review, so I have lots of things to say about later book events. I have put them in spoiler sections, but there are a lot of spoiler sections. My opinion may be clouded by my strange emotional reaction, so don't judge... </blockquote> </b> </p><br /> <p> My reaction to this one was even stranger than the last one. The beginning was very hard to get through due to the writing style and how much the main character took to wailing. It gave an incredibly childish vibe, as if someone very young had written it <i>(Later note : I learned this was a translated book, so I feel a bit bad for sharing this opinion but I'm going to maintain it, as a mild warning towards people less used to reading perhaps not perfectly translated books)</i> and I rather disliked the first part. </p><br /> <p> The second part... Just turned incredibly depressing. Really the whole book gave a depressing vibe. Like an idiotic human race has taken over the world and hunted everything else to extinction, and then taken it upon themselves to make everyone miserable for eternity. Was a very gloomy read; I really need to find a more positive book for my mood swingy <i>(definitely a word, that swingy)</i> brain to intake. </p><br /><p> The characters...? <br /> Well, the main character, Yorsh <i>(reading his full name so many times was difficulty, I'm not bothering)</i> was, in the first part, so childish and whiny I wanted to read another book, but in the second part I suppose I may have felt very bad for him, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[or else felt bad for everyone who knew him, because I think he's bad luck. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /> Monser and Sajra seemed cool enough, I supposed, but their relationship was very odd... <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ First, Monser's pointing a bow at Sajra, then they take a casual adventure together to protect an annoying elf, then as they're leaving one makes some comment along the lines of "If we had a kid I'd name them-" then the other responds with "You mean <i>when</i> we <i>have</i> a kid ;)" and then it skips 13 years forward and they're dead and their child is miserable . . . <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /> The dog is cute, and loyal, and of course I love dogs. He was perfectly worth the time, because dogs are adorable. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ And of course they have to die dramatically proving that they're loyal enough to wait 13 years for someone to come back to them...  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /> The dragons are regular enough, arrogant and rather annoying but all the while somewhat lovable. Although, I'd put more emphasis on "rather annoying." <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and all the while destined to die, don't get attached. I got attached... It was of course a final, heroic exit... <b> Chokes up </b>  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> </p><br /><p> </p><blockquote> <i> Okay, okay, I think my emotional rant is over. Overall, the book wasn't too bad, I guess... Just, it was long and depressing for the age range, but the writing style and more general themes did fit a children's book. I don't know if I'm perfectly happy with all the time spent reading this book and the outcome, but it definitely left an impression... </i> </blockquote> </span>

<div id="review_2035894307" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2035894307" />
    <a title="Adela Bezemer-Cleverley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5658907-adela-bezemer-cleverley"><img alt="Adela Bezemer-Cleverley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442598220p2/5658907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2035894307?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adela Bezemer-Cleverley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adela Bezemer-Cleverley" href="/user/show/5658907-adela-bezemer-cleverley">Adela Bezemer-Cleverley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5658907-adela-bezemer-cleverley?shelf=all-stars">all-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5658907-adela-bezemer-cleverley?shelf=children">children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2035894307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17810417651986402568">This book is impeccable. This book is just so freaking charming.<br/><br/>A fairy tale-esque middle-grade fantasy about a dragon and a young elf at the beginning of their journey of rescuing a country from a violent and selfish overlord whose people are treated like slaves and punished for the injustices done to them, while also being starved and separated from their families. That's a good summary, but this book is more than that: almost from the beginning I was thinking about how perfect this book is fo</span>
  <span id="freeText17810417651986402568" style="display:none">This book is impeccable. This book is just so freaking charming.<br /><br />A fairy tale-esque middle-grade fantasy about a dragon and a young elf at the beginning of their journey of rescuing a country from a violent and selfish overlord whose people are treated like slaves and punished for the injustices done to them, while also being starved and separated from their families. That's a good summary, but this book is more than that: almost from the beginning I was thinking about how perfect this book is for children to read or have read to them--in fact it has jumped right to the top of my list of "books to read to my students". <i>The Last Dragon</i> is so well written and the lessons it teaches come from the empathy you feel for the characters.<br /><br />Whether readers recognize the significance or not, it's a story of impoverished and terrorized group of people escaping from a fascist regime as refugees. It's about one last member of a minority group that has been subject to genocide (the elves--first relocated to Elf Camps and then quietly starved and hanged and drowned out of existence) leading a revolution of people who have been brainwashed all their lives to believe that elves are evil and cruel and responsible for all of the bad in the world. It's about refugees escaping a life of poverty and labour and mud and beatings to build a better life for themselves on the shores of the sea. If it wasn't written with such delightful and light-hearted language it would definitely be way too dark to be a children's book.<br /><br />And, it's also completely adorable. The second half of the book gets a bit darker and more action-packed and emotional, but in the first half of the story we meet Yorsh and he's this little elf child (a little one, a puppy, one born lately), coming out of a terrible situation and still being so innocent and positive and delighted by the little good things that happen, while also being dramatically devastated about all the little bad things (and devastatingly quiet about the big bad things). Right from the beginning you see him coming into his powers, his ability to turn people's lives around and make them see the good in themselves and what's worth fighting for. <br /><br />And then there's a dragon and a library and the accumulation of knowledge and isolation and re-learning empathy and rescue missions and a little orphan girl who turns out to be a bit of a warrior queen underneath all of that dirt and sadness, and there's rats and butterflies and little animals being brought back to life and people trekking out of hardship and into happiness and the potential for many more adventures.<br /><br />It really is a very simple story when you think about it, but it can make the reader (or listener, if one is being read to) feel so many things, and learn so many things, all while being perfectly charming and lovely in terms of writing and imagery and everything. It is quite sad, a lot of it, and made me cry a couple of times actually, but it is also a story full of hope. I look forward to reading the rest of the series!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17810417651986402568" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2309681773" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2309681773" />
    <a title="Ellie C." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73329718-ellie-c"><img alt="Ellie C." src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2309681773?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ellie C." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ellie C." href="/user/show/73329718-ellie-c">Ellie C.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2309681773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10368581407354659277">The book The Last Dragon, is about an elf, a dog, a man, a woman, and, of course, a dragon. Part I tells the story of an elf named Yorsh. In a land full of famine, crime, and rain, Yorsh is the last elf, a little one, a puppy, one born lately, (Inside Joke), who has never been exposed to human culture. When he meets the man and woman, Moser and Sajra, and the dog, who he names Fido, they get into lots of trouble together. They discover a prophecy, foretelling that the last elf and the last drago</span>
  <span id="freeText10368581407354659277" style="display:none">The book The Last Dragon, is about an elf, a dog, a man, a woman, and, of course, a dragon. Part I tells the story of an elf named Yorsh. In a land full of famine, crime, and rain, Yorsh is the last elf, a little one, a puppy, one born lately, (Inside Joke), who has never been exposed to human culture. When he meets the man and woman, Moser and Sajra, and the dog, who he names Fido, they get into lots of trouble together. They discover a prophecy, foretelling that the last elf and the last dragon will end the rains and famine, they go in search of the last dragon, and Yorsh promises him that he will stay with him for the rest of his life, and Sajra and Moser leave. Part II takes place thirteen years later, when Yorsh discovers his dragon has and egg, and tries to fulfill the second part of the prophecy. The daughter of Sajra and Moser, Robi, is now an orphan who must help Yorsh defeat the tyrant government and bring a group of refugees to safety.<br /><br />Because of the two parts, pinpointing the main conflict was a little hard, but I finally decided that the main conflict is escaping or fulfilling fate, and how to live with past choices and events. The pressure of the prophecy on Yorsh, Sajra, Moser, and Robi is crushing. Yorsh promised to spend his entire life on a secluded mountain top with a grumpy dragon to supposedly save the world, and he is sick of it. Because the second part of the prophecy talks about Yorsh's bride, and the fate of the last dragon, it causes some interesting controversy. Robi is unsure if the bride is her, Yorsh is uncertain if she wants it to be her. The dragon, Erbrow the Younger wishes with all his might he could abandon the crazy elf, the scraggly band of rebels, and Robi, who is dubbed the ragged queen, but something is stopping him. They all know they have a role to play, but they have no little to no idea what it is, how they should do it, or when it should happen. Therefore, confusion, miscommunication, mistrust, and overall chaos ensues. Because of the adversity, they learn that they are free to follow whichever way they choose, because the prophecy has already foreseen that they would do it regardless of what it says. <br /><br />Personally I thought this book was amazing. The two different parts, years apart, allowed for some amazing detail work and world building. The teeniest things matched up. In part I, Yorsh is probably only five or six, and he grew up outside of human culture, leading to some hilarious mistakes in language, custom, and culture. Some of his common mistakes, or inside jokes with Sajra and Monser were not only mentioned in abundance in part I, but also carried over to part II. The detail of the characters, world, and plot were so well thought out and intricate. I loved it so much!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10368581407354659277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_311196979" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/311196979" />
    <a title="Serena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7277052-serena"><img alt="Serena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454500339p2/7277052.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/311196979?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Serena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Serena" href="/user/show/7277052-serena">Serena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788884514011" href="/book/show/9077765-l-ultimo-elfo">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7277052-serena?shelf=this-is-the-shit">this-is-the-shit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer311196979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18099317113230389379">(Mi sono ripromessa di non rendere questa recensione troppo lunga – anche perchè poi mi toccherà tradurla, ma non prometto niente: non sono sempre coerente, quando si tratta di questo libro – I promised myself not to make this review too long – also because I’ll have to translate it later, but I won’t guarantee it: I’m not always coherent, when it comes to this book)<br/>ENGLISH TRANSLATION AT THE BOTTOM! NOT SPOILER FREE!!<br/>Se non ricordo male, ho letto questo libro per la prima volta quando avevo cir</span>
  <span id="freeText18099317113230389379" style="display:none">(Mi sono ripromessa di non rendere questa recensione troppo lunga – anche perchè poi mi toccherà tradurla, ma non prometto niente: non sono sempre coerente, quando si tratta di questo libro – I promised myself not to make this review too long – also because I’ll have to translate it later, but I won’t guarantee it: I’m not always coherent, when it comes to this book)<br />ENGLISH TRANSLATION AT THE BOTTOM! NOT SPOILER FREE!!<br />Se non ricordo male, ho letto questo libro per la prima volta quando avevo circa nove anni. Avrebbe dovuto durarmi per tutte le vacanze estive, eppure nel mio miglior stile, l’avevo praticamente finito in una giornata. Ricordo ancora quell’infinito viaggio in macchina per andare in Sicilia, e come fossi assolutamente incapace di smettere di leggere. Alti e bassi e poi di nuovo alti, ed io che piangevo come una scema nel sedile di dietro, sperando che nessuno se ne accorgesse [SPOILER] è stata la morte di Fido, tra tutte le cose che avrebbero potuto farmi piangere, a farmi crollare completamente[SPOILER]<br />Sono passati... quanto, otto anni? Otto anni, ed ancora questo libro occupa un posto speciale nel mio cuore. Per anni ho ignorato l’esistenza di un sequel (figuriamoci quattro!) e nonostante ciò, non ho mai smesso di amarlo.<br />Non credo che esista un altro singolo libro che mi abbia influenzata a questa maniera (Harry Potter non conta – ho sempre saputo che sarebbero stati sette).<br />Non vi ricapitolerò la trama – probabilmente la sapete già, avendo letto il bel riassuntino là sopra. Quello che vi dirò è: ho amato questo libro, in tutti i suoi difetti.<br />Li ho sentiti elencare: è un libro da bambini. La morale è troppo pesante. Eccetera, eccetera.<br />So che non a tutti piacerà questo libro, ma a me ha sinceramente cambiato la vita. Indipendentemente dai sequel (l’Ultimo Orco l’ho amato, Gli Ultimi Incantesimi è stato bello, l’Ultima Profezia mi ha deluso molto). <br />Posso ancora riconoscere le influenze dell’Ultimo Elfo nel mio modo di scrivere, nei temi e tipi di personaggi che prediligo. <br />Ho riletto questo libro innumerevoli volte eppure ogni volta sorrido al vocabolario stentato di Yorsh che diventa sempre più preciso, finchè non è lui stesso a correggere gli umani. Ogni volta rido al sarcasmo pungente di Monser a Sajra, ogni volta mi si spezza il cuore quando sono costretti a lasciare Yorsh (soprattutto ora che so che non si riicontreranno mai più). Ogni volta soffro ed esulto assieme a Robi (Robi! Il mio personaggio femminile preferito di tutti i tempi!). Ogni volta mi riinnamoro di Yorsh attraverso i suoi occhi, ed ogni volta aspetto, tendendomi con ogni fibra del mio essere, che si mettano insieme (purtroppo nemmeno un bacio – e nonostante tutto sono, anche loro, una delle mie coppie preferite di tutti i tempi). Ogni volta mi commuovo per ciascuna delle morti (ogni tanto mi vengono gli occhi lucidi per Fido, anche se non ho più pianto, dopo la prima volta). Ogni volta provo quell’assurdo senso di disperazione e speranza e trionfo nella marcia degli straccioni verso il mare. <br />A volte voglio poter credere, come la prima volta, che finisca lì – che siano felici per sempre su quella spiaggia e che si ricostruiscano quei futuri luminosi che riuscivo ad immaginare per loro, quando avevo nove anni.<br />Ma ora so. E nonostante soffra come un cane a pensare a quante sofferenze ancora avranno da sopportare (i miei bambini!), non posso rammaricarmi di aver letto l’Ultimo Orco.<br />Ho amato questo libro – da morire. <br />Ed era prima di Rankstrail. Prima di conoscere la vera Aurora. Prima di Erbrow la Mezza Elfo. Prima dell’assurdo, terribile, magnifico arco che ha trasformato Robi nella Regina del Popolo degli Uomini.<br />Non so se sarà capace di cambiare la vostra vita come ha cambiato la mia – parte della ragione per cui l’ha fatto è dovuta di sicuro alla mia età quando l’ho letto la prima volta. Ma sono certa che, per lo meno, non vi pentirete di averlo letto.<br /><br />[ENGLISH]<br /><br />If I remember correctly, I first read this book when I was about nine years old. It was supposed to last me through the summer, and yet, in my best style, I’d practically finished it in a day. I still remember that never ending drive to Sicily, and how I was completely unable to stop reading. Highs and lows and highs again, and me crying like a fool in the backseat, hoping that no one would notice [SPOILER] it was Fido’s death, of all things, that broke me down [SPOILER]<br />It’s been… what, eight years? Eight years, and this book still holds a special place in my heart. For years I ignored the existence of a sequel (let alone four!) and despite that, I never stopped loving it.<br />I don’t think there is a single book that influenced me this way (Harry Potter doesn’t count – I always knew they would be seven).<br />I won’t recap the plot – you probably know it already, having read the pretty synopsis up there. What I will tell you is: I loved this book, with all its flaws.<br />I’ve heard them all: it’s a children’s book. The morale is too stressed. And so on and so forth.<br />Not everybody will like this book, but it sincerely changed my life. Regardless of its sequels (The Last Ogre loved, The Last Spells was beautiful, The Last Prophecy disappointed me greatly).<br />I can still spot this book’s influences in my writing, in the themes and types of characters I prefer.<br />I re-read this book countless times, and yet each time I smile at Yorsh’s broken vocabulary, that becomes more and more precise until he is the one correcting the humans. Each time I laugh at Monser and Sajra’s biting sarcasm, each time my heart breaks when they are forced to leave Yorsh (especially now that I know they won’t ever meet again). Each time I suffer and cheer with Robi (Robi! My favorite female character of all time!). Each time I fall back in love with Yorsh through her eyes, and each time I wait, tensing with every fiber of my being, for them to be together (sadly not even a kiss – and despite this they too are one of my favorite couples of all time). Each time I’m moved by every one of the deaths (sometimes I still tear up for Fido, although I never cried again, after that first time). Each time I feel that absurd sense of desperation and hope and triumph for the beggars’ march towards the sea.<br />Sometimes I want to be able to believe, like the first time, that it ends there – that they’re forever happy on that beach and that they’ll build themselves those bright futures I could imagine for them, when I was nine years old.<br />But now I know. And despite the fact that I suffer like crazy in thinking about how much suffering they still have to endure (my babies!), I can’t regret reading the Last Ogre.<br />I loved this book – to death.<br />And this was before Rankstrail. Before knowing the real Aurora (Dawn in the English version, I think). Before little Erbrow. Before that absurd, terrible, magnificent arc that turned Robi into the Queen of the Human People.<br />I don’t know it will be able to change your lives like it did mine – part of the reason why it did is due to my age when I first read it. But I’m sure that, at least, you won’t regret reading it.<br /><br />[Sadly, I think there’s no English translation for any of the sequels – it’s a pity. It’s starting with the first sequel that this turns from a children’s fairy tale into pure high fantasy (the type with wars and witches burned at the stake, and heads chopped off and stuck on pikes!) and maybe that’s why they never did. Too jarring of a tone shift? Poor sensitive English-speaking readers *eyeroll*]<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18099317113230389379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3098366334&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3098366334" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"

<span id="freeTextContainer13390788823058120311">Very good book. I love stories where almost all questions are answered and perspectives are explained. <br/><br/>Theres always a reason the characters act the way they do- the elf is driven by emotion and belief in life, the humans are driven by needs or greed, and the dragon is driven by his righteous nature. Each hold each other in contempt or in fear of each other due to their perspective of each other’s actions. I liked the mindset of the elf the best, as contrary as it seems to the others.<br/><br/>I think my </span>
  <span id="freeText13390788823058120311" style="display:none">Very good book. I love stories where almost all questions are answered and perspectives are explained. <br /><br />Theres always a reason the characters act the way they do- the elf is driven by emotion and belief in life, the humans are driven by needs or greed, and the dragon is driven by his righteous nature. Each hold each other in contempt or in fear of each other due to their perspective of each other’s actions. I liked the mindset of the elf the best, as contrary as it seems to the others.<br /><br />I think my favorite part is MAJOR SPOILER HERE when the dragon perishes and his perspective in that “you can’t live century after century nurturing your own magnificence and your own loneliness” and that “what is important is not things, but the meaning we give to things” (346). The different messages through the book are very moving. One of my other favorite themes was how stories have power and can adapt. Page 331 starts out with the elf telling a story and the story sustaining the people through the grueling journey and gave them courage to face the army, their last major obstacle.<br /><br />I gave it four stars due to the jarring time jumps. Part one and part two have 13 year time difference due to a dragon reason, and there is a new character introduced almost out of nowhere at the start of part two, which is not explained till several chapters later how she is relevant. I was able to follow along, but it was a bit frustrating. As a complete work, it is an amazing story.</span>

  <span id="freeText9903556500201061646" style="display:none">There are words for things like this:<br /><br />Astounding. Magnificent. Hilarious. A journey well worth taking--and then more, far beyond that.<br /><br />Once again I have occasion to be grateful for the literary trail my daughter Andrea left when she got married and moved out a few years ago. If she hadn't left behind this book, I'm not sure if I ever would have discovered it. Oh, and I would have been missing out on some gorgeous prose, a truly unique story-line, and a load of laughs besides. If Ursula K. LeGuin were a humorist, I would say that Silvana de Mari is the Italian LeGuin.<br /><br />Now, the translation by Shaun Whiteside, although top-notch is slightly disconcerting here and there. Apparently Mr. Whiteside doesn't know the difference between 'millenia' and 'millenium' . . . ? And then the occurrence of "get ahold of" in at least two spots. That "redneckism" definitely doesn't belong in a formal narrative. However, I'm not letting those tiny drawbacks keep from giving the book five stars. If I could give it SEVEN stars, I would.</span>

<div id="review_2020689243" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2020689243" />
    <a title="Shanepruett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47725497-shanepruett"><img alt="Shanepruett" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2020689243?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shanepruett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shanepruett" href="/user/show/47725497-shanepruett">Shanepruett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2020689243" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2450005819615185798">I read this along with my 9 year old daughter. She became very engrossed in this bittersweet, but humorous tale of the last elf and his association with the last dragon. There is much sadness and I was afraid my daughter would find it overwhelming at times, but she took it in stride and thoroughly enjoyed the book. There is loss, imaginative history and hints at how things came to be. This book in particular opened a few great discussions for us about the importance of keeping and sharing knowle</span>
  <span id="freeText2450005819615185798" style="display:none">I read this along with my 9 year old daughter. She became very engrossed in this bittersweet, but humorous tale of the last elf and his association with the last dragon. There is much sadness and I was afraid my daughter would find it overwhelming at times, but she took it in stride and thoroughly enjoyed the book. There is loss, imaginative history and hints at how things came to be. This book in particular opened a few great discussions for us about the importance of keeping and sharing knowledge, friendship and loyalty, and the pains of letting go of those we love. I recommend it for parents wanting to be able to discuss some of these weighty topics with young kids who've reached the age of reason. The humor does a great job of balancing the heft of the topics. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2450005819615185798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2168849516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11466362228508354423">This book really surprised me. Despite being for children it didn't pull any punches when it comes to death or human cruelty. For example, a lot of the events and themes remind me of books about the Holocaust -- the indoctrination about "enemies", prejudice/hatred towards the elves, the rounding up into "elf camps", and genocide. <br/><br/>I would definitely recommend giving this book a read. The only disappointing thing about this book is that it's a series and the rest of the series is not available in </span>
  <span id="freeText11466362228508354423" style="display:none">This book really surprised me. Despite being for children it didn't pull any punches when it comes to death or human cruelty. For example, a lot of the events and themes remind me of books about the Holocaust -- the indoctrination about "enemies", prejudice/hatred towards the elves, the rounding up into "elf camps", and genocide. <br /><br />I would definitely recommend giving this book a read. The only disappointing thing about this book is that it's a series and the rest of the series is not available in English. (The series is originally published in 2004 in Italian, however, only the first book is translated to English.) </span>
  <a data-text-id="11466362228508354423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tyaGzVcdYg8QtmeR8Nf0jsnY1Wayz9hRzuBtuH6NkIvVfGkxh3bDNMHpm8S7Z8/1fNuuf5FSUstozRoh1/33yQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mxN1O3hAuORwxHByN+3TuzL7Xs5uRZQgp8vbeaZXG0T5SZrHqCsZ36GbjCd8XejAh/gl103YHroB5qzgDyd8Bg==')})
  }
//]]>

Ma questo giovane insegnante è diverso: una luce gli brilla negli occhi quando sprona gli studenti a cercare il proprio sogno. Leo ha un nemico che lo atterrisce: il bianco. \nIl bianco è l’assenza, tutto ciò che ha a che fare con la privazione e la perdita è bianco. \nIl rosso invece è il colore dell’amore, della passione, del sangue; rosso è il colore dei capelli di Beatrice. Perché un sogno Leo ce l’ha e si chiama Beatrice, anche se lei ancora non lo sa. Leo ha anche una realtà, Silvia, una presenza affidabile e serena. \nQuando scopre che Beatrice è ammalata e che la malattia ha qualcosa a che fare con quel bianco che tanto lo spaventa, Leo dovrà scavare a fondo dentro di sé, sanguinare e rinascere, per capire che i sogni non possono morire e trovare il coraggio di credere in qualcosa di più grande.<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11513489843198113189">Silvana de Mari, medico chirurgo, ha lavorato in Italia e, come volontaria, in Etiopia. Da quando le è venuto il dubbio che i mali dell’anima siano devastanti quanto quelli del corpo, si occupa di psicoterapia. Per Salani ha pubblicato negli Istrici L’ultima stella a destra della luna, La bestia e la bella e L’ultimo Elfo (Premio Andersen 2004), che l’ha consacrata star internazionale della fantas</span>
  <span id="freeText11513489843198113189" style="display:none">Silvana de Mari, medico chirurgo, ha lavorato in Italia e, come volontaria, in Etiopia. Da quando le è venuto il dubbio che i mali dell’anima siano devastanti quanto quelli del corpo, si occupa di psicoterapia. Per Salani ha pubblicato negli Istrici L’ultima stella a destra della luna, La bestia e la bella e L’ultimo Elfo (Premio Andersen 2004), che l’ha consacrata star internazionale della fantasy: tradotta in diciotto lingue, Silvana De Mari è l’autore italiano più venduto nel mondo dopo Camilleri. Da Salani sono usciti inoltre L’ultimo Orco (2006), secondo libro della saga iniziata con L’ultimo Elfo, e il saggio sulla fantasy Il drago come realtà. Del 2009 è Il gatto dagli occhi d'oro e Il cavaliere, la strega, la morte e il diavolo.<br /></span>

Gli ultimi incantesimi è una storia di eroi involontari, improvvisati, minori, quelli bassi, i brutti, i deboli, i qualsiasi. È un coro di voci piccole.
